  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_921651368" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/921651368" />
    <a title="Kara Babcock" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1188122-kara-babcock"><img alt="Kara Babcock" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1580776593p2/1188122.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/921651368?book_show_action=true">Apr 27, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kara Babcock" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kara Babcock" href="/user/show/1188122-kara-babcock">Kara Babcock</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780765365682" href="/book/show/22320446-the-goblin-emperor">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=from-library">from-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=2015-read">2015-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=hugo-nominee">hugo-nominee</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer921651368" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17907494913837292927">Nearly two years ago, I read a book by Sarah Monette called <em>Mélusine</em>, and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/55854941' rel='nofollow noopener'>I hated it</a>. I considered it a train wreck of a novel. I wasn’t looking to read anything more by Monette in a long time. Now she’s back under the pen name of Katherine Addison (apparently for career reasons, which is a little silly, but I can also understand why). And not only am I giving <em>The Goblin Emperor</em> four stars, but I consider it every bit worth the Hugo nomination it has received, and I will not be disappointed if i</span>
  <span id="freeText17907494913837292927" style="display:none">Nearly two years ago, I read a book by Sarah Monette called <em>Mélusine</em>, and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/55854941" rel="nofollow noopener">I hated it</a>. I considered it a train wreck of a novel. I wasn’t looking to read anything more by Monette in a long time. Now she’s back under the pen name of Katherine Addison (apparently for career reasons, which is a little silly, but I can also understand why). And not only am I giving <em>The Goblin Emperor</em> four stars, but I consider it every bit worth the Hugo nomination it has received, and I will not be disappointed if it wins. (I’m not sure if I’m voting for it yet, because I haven’t read <em>The Three Body Problem</em> or <em>Ancillary Sword</em> and expect good things from both.) This is a novel that is worth its hype.<br><br>Having had a few days now to think on it, what has stayed with me the most about <em>The Goblin Emperor</em> is its <em>positivity</em>. Don’t get me wrong: plenty of terrible things happen in this book. Maia spends his entire life prior to becoming emperor in seclusion, raised by an abusive and negligent disgraced courtier and kept ignorant of the political and social knowledge he desperately needs if he is to rule. After he becomes emperor, he is totally the victim of a couple of coup attempts—plus he doesn’t know how to dance!<br><br>No, what I mean is that the arc of the plot is quite positive: things for Maia (and the empire itself) gradually and steadily get better. The setbacks are more like growing pains. For a book about an inexperienced young half-breed becoming emperor, I expected a lot worse to happen. I expected open rebellion, a forced marriage to a woman he doesn’t love, and complete inability to change anything about the empire or court. Instead, Maia manages to make friends, deal effectively with some of his enemies, and make a start towards real change. He is a surprisingly effective ruler.<br><br>Why does this positivity surprise me? It shouldn’t, really. But I feel like a large proportion of recent fantasy has gone to the dark side. We can blame grimdark (or is that GRRMdark?) for some of this trend—being “edgy” is cool (but writing rape scenes is <em>not</em> cool, <em>Game of Thrones</em> writers…). And perhaps it’s a reflection of current events, the zeitgeist, whatever…. That’s a larger discussion that we might be able to have here. Whatever the reason, dark is cool and light not so much, so in this climate, <em>The Goblin Emperor</em> is a surprise and a delight and a breath of fresh air.<br><br>Addison shows that positive fiction can still be paltable, even pleasurable fiction. Despite the fact that Maia’s closest allies don’t somehow end up dead in the middle of Act Three to really “raise the stakes” before the conclusion, I still managed to enjoy the book. I guess I’m just a freak or something. Anyway, the pleasure in this book comes from a few sources—your individual mileage with each source is going to vary greatly, but I think this is a fantasy novel that could appeal to a wide range of fantasy fans.<br><br>The worldbuilding, for one, is top notch. I was very critical of the way Monette manages the worldbuilding in <em>Mélusine</em>. It’s much better here. The story might be about “elves” and “goblins,” but there is almost no magic involved (I think there are <em>two</em> scenes total that involve explicit magic use). It’s all court intrigue and politics. Addison uses a consistent system of prefixes, suffixes, etc., in names of people and places to help create the sense that this is an old, proud society. For those of you who get tripped up with this stuff, or want to know more about it, there is an appendix (a great way to avoid too much exposition). For those like me whose eyes just skim over the stuff one thinks unimportant, the consistency of these devices makes it easy to ignore them once you understand what’s going on.<br><br>Maia’s vulnerability balances perfectly with his proactive nature as a protagonist. He is not the Chosen One by any stretch of the imagination (pretty much no one wants him as emperor, not even him, but that’s what happens when you have an inherited system of governance!). Yet he is not quite toothless either. The whole first act is basically him <em>reacting</em> and relying on people like Csevet (whom I kept expecting to betray him, because grimdark) to give him advice. In Act Two, Maia starts testing the waters: he starts making allies, making decisions, taking action, generally <em>being emperor</em>. Then in Act Three, the baddies push back, launch their own plans, and Maia and his allies have to deal with the fallout. During this whole time, Maia is evaluating his actions and others’ and changing as a person as he tries to become the emperor he thinks this country needs.<br><br>This emphasis on Maia’s <em>duty as the emperor</em> is excellent, because it is so multi-faceted. Everyone has a taken on it. Maia’s servants and minions feel that he has a duty to present a certain persona. He can’t apologize, for instance—that’s not emperor-like! Chavar the Lord Chancellor expects Maia to be a puppet emperor, unschooled and naive enough for him to manipulate or bypass completely. Tethimar certainly hopes Maia will be a weak emperor easily swayed by veild (or not-so-veiled) threats. And Maia himself feels that he has a duty to <em>not be his father</em>, who was cold and disapproving of most things Maia approves of (like his mother). Much of the conflict of this book comes from Maia’s attempts to negotiate the tensions among these various interpretations of his duty.<br><br>As a consequence, Addison produces an adept commentary on the challenges faced by a parliamentary monarch. In addition to the central question—what makes a good monarch?—she explores the difficulty becoming a monarch and maintaining one’s own individuality—the distinction between Maia, a person with wants and needs, and Edrehasivar VII, the emperor. Scores of historical fiction novels tackle this theme, but I feel like fantasy tends to get caught up in the <em>action</em> around monarchs more so than their introspective, personal selves. It becomes all about the attempts to overthrow a ruler, or install a different ruler, or take back a throne that was usurped, or defend against invaders, etc. Addison reminds us that we don’t have to be philosophers, or even load a story down with overly philosophical language, in order to consider the morals and ethics of ruling while we care about the person becoming a ruler.<br><br>I love when a book convinces me to change my mind about something. That’s why I read and read widely. In this case, my first experience with this author wasn’t a positive one. A couple years later, a different book (and, let’s face it, a different <em>me</em>), and now I’m a fan. I want more. Write more books like this please. I don’t care if they are set in the world. Just write more.<br><br><a href="http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/4.0/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img alt="Creative Commons BY-NC License" width="88" height="31" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1388723110i/7909581.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="17907494913837292927" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_868470478" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/868470478" />
    <a title="Kristen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8409451-kristen"><img alt="Kristen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1513342355p2/8409451.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/868470478?book_show_action=true">Mar 01, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kristen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kristen" href="/user/show/8409451-kristen">Kristen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8409451-kristen?shelf=fantasy-is-my-jam">fantasy-is-my-jam</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer868470478" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9198514595795777237">3.5/5 stars<br/><br/>WARNING: This review is pretty long (and also quite boring). I didn’t write a novel on purpose, there were just so many aspects I felt like I needed to mention. OKAY!<br/><br/><br/><i>"After a time, he felt a deeper rhythm, the rhythm of the stone and water, not the rhythm of his words and heartbeat. He breathed into this deeper rhythm, let it teach him a new mantra, a wordless mantra that waxed and waned, ebbed and flowed, moon and stars and clouds, river and sun, the wordless singing of the earth ben</i></span>
  <span id="freeText9198514595795777237" style="display:none">3.5/5 stars<br /><br />WARNING: This review is pretty long (and also quite boring). I didn’t write a novel on purpose, there were just so many aspects I felt like I needed to mention. OKAY!<br /><br /><br /><i>"After a time, he felt a deeper rhythm, the rhythm of the stone and water, not the rhythm of his words and heartbeat. He breathed into this deeper rhythm, let it teach him a new mantra, a wordless mantra that waxed and waned, ebbed and flowed, moon and stars and clouds, river and sun, the wordless singing of the earth beneath it all like the world's own heartbeat. He laid his palms flat on the stone beneath him and listened in quiet rapture to the mantra of the world's praying."</i> <br /><br /><br /><b>The skinny:</b><br /><br />Maia, a goblin who was sent away by his father and almost forgotten, has found himself as the new Emperor of Ethuveraz. His position is obtained after his father and half brothers were killed in an airship crash. While Maia is trying to navigate the ins and outs of his new, unfamiliar, position he discovers that his father and brothers were in fact not just killed, but murdered. Maia must learn to be the emperor that his people want, while trying to find the details behind the death of his family members.<br /><br /><b>The review</b>:<br /><br /><u>Writing:</u><br /><br />This book was beautifully written. It was obvious that the author put a lot of time and effort into the creation of the characters, countries, etc., of her world. I really loved reading this story because of how well written it was. If I can expect the same level of writing in all of Katherine Addison’s books, then I will definitely be giving her future works a shot. <br /><br /><u>Plot:</u><br /><br />When it comes to fantasy, I typically lean towards books that have a good mix of politics and action. This book definitely leaned towards the more political aspect of fantasy; there was very little action, and the action that was present was slow paced and not overly exciting. This being said, I really enjoyed the slow pace of the book and getting to know the ins and outs of the political world that Addison created.<br /><br />I feel that the synopsis of this book is a tad misleading. It states that Maia’s dad and half brothers were killed in an airship crash, and he eventually discovers that his family members were in fact murdered. I feel that the synopsis really plays up the murder angle of the story and makes it seem as if the entire book is going to be about discovering who the culprits of the murders were. In fact, for about three quarters of the book, this aspect of the plot was merely in the background; there were many other plot points that took place that were more at the forefront of the story. Probably the largest portion of the story revolved around Maia becoming acclimated to his role as emperor.<br /><br /><u>Characters:</u> <br /><br />The first thing that must be said is that the character list is very extensive. There is one main character, Maia, yet there are dozens of secondary characters (some more important/more present than others). The amount of characters coupled with the complicated and similar names makes it hard to keep the different characters straight (example of similar names: Chavada, Chavar , Chavel). Thankfully, there is an appendix at the end of the book that offers a list of all the different character names, towns, countries, etc. I had to visit the appendix many times, because I got several of the characters confused. It also took me awhile to work out the various prefixes used to denote sex and marital status. Once again, these prefixes are explained at the back of the book. Overall it took me about half of the book to have a good grasp of who was who.<br /><br />Maia: <br /><br />Maia is the main character in the story. He comes to the role of emperor as an abused, awkward, and neglected young man. Having been generally unschooled in the ways of the aristocracy, Maia makes many mistakes when interacting with his noble ‘peers.’ For the majority of the book, Maia revels in his inability to be a good emperor, and, at times, wallows in self-reflection (this often seems like self-pity). Maia began to irritate me about half-way through the book due to his hesitant attitude. A little past the half-way mark, Maia started coming into his own. While he was still hesitant and uncomfortable with his role as Emperor, Maia began to make decisions for his kingdom, and stood his ground on issues he found to be important. Overall I liked Maia. He was a realistic character who struggled with being thrown into such a prolific position. Despite being annoyed by him, I was really excited when Maia started to take the reins and lead his people.<br /><br />I felt very badly for Maia for most of the novel. I felt for him because he was thrust into the position of emperor having little to no experience with politics and court life. Not only this, but many of the people he was surrounded by were loyal to his father and not him. This made Maia’s job even harder because he was trying to work with/please people who had not even given him a chance. Some of these people hated Maia so strongly that they were willing to attempt to take his life.<br /><br /><b>Overall:</b> <br /><br />This book is not for everyone; it is wordy, at times confusing, and slow paced. If you prefer fantasy that encompasses a fast-paced, action riddled plot, then this book would not be for you. If you enjoy fantasy novels that delve into the intricacies of politics, then you should give this one a shot!<br /><br />P.S. This book is categorized as being young adult on Goodreads. I don’t really consider this YA at all, not because of any graphic occurrences, but because it is pretty confusing. I mean, I had trouble keeping all of the characters straight, so I imagine a younger person would as well!<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9198514595795777237" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('sFd0UzU133u0HpazzPIkB7iU+OGzHfXEXFlAD+9QSs9cUf+A5n4Sm3xwNk2KSRYuUEjMhKbr5/WYySTqltICaA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

  <span id="freeText11220533765631442327" style="display:none">A pseudonym of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/128570.Sarah_Monette" title="Sarah Monette" rel="nofollow noopener">Sarah Monette</a>. Both Sarah and Katherine are on Twitter as <a href="https://twitter.com/pennyvixen" rel="nofollow noopener">@pennyvixen</a>. Katherine reviews nonfiction. Sarah reviews fiction. Fair warning: I read very little fiction these days. <br /><br />Sarah/Katherine was born and raised in Oak Ridge, Tennessee, one of the three secret cities of the Manhattan Project.<br /><br />She got her B.A. from Case Western Reserve University, her M.A. and Ph.D. from the University of Wisconsin-Madison. Despite being <em>summa cum laude</em>, none of her degrees is of the slightest use to her in either her day job or her writing, which she feels is an object lesson for us all.<br /><br />She currently lives near Madison, Wisconsin.</span>

And we wish them trapped there as we were trapped.' <br /><br />'You consider that unjust, Serenity?' <br /><br />'We consider it cruel,' Maia said. 'And we do not think that cruelty is ever just.' &rdquo;</span>
